The Fourth Proof: Humanity's Desperate Need for Guidance
Enumerates corruption of nations and argues for necessity of a prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the widespread corruption and misguidance before the Prophet's mission serve as a proof of his prophethood and necessity from divine wisdom.

Core Idea
The world's corruption proved the urgent need for a prophet—and Muhammad ﷺ was the answer.

This lesson examines the fourth proof from classical kalam: the condition of the world at the time of the Prophet's mission. The author paints a vivid picture of moral and spiritual decay across civilizations, then asks a rhetorical question that leads to the conclusion that only a divinely sent guide could restore order. This argument highlights both the necessity of prophethood and the unique suitability of Muhammad ﷺ.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the author's description of pre-Islamic corruption.
Notice the vivid language: 'valleys of misguidance', 'tents of delusion'.
2
Identify the condition of each nation
List the specific corruptions mentioned for each group: Arabs, Persians, Turks, Indians, Jews, Christians, and others.
The author uses this enumeration to show the universality of corruption.
3
Analyze the rhetorical question
Consider the rhetorical question: 'Is it befitting the wisdom of the clear true King not to send a mercy to the worlds?' This is the core argument from divine wisdom.
The question assumes that a wise and merciful God would not leave humanity in such a state.
4
Connect to the Prophet's lineage
The author concludes by naming the Prophet Muhammad with his full lineage, emphasizing his suitability as the one sent.
The lineage establishes his noble ancestry, a point of credibility in Arab culture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: أنه ظهر أحوج ما كان الناس إلى من يهدي إلى الطريق المستقيم، ويدعو إلى الدين العويم، وينظم الأمور، ويضبط حال الجمهور لكونه زمان فترة من الرسل، وتفرق للسبل وانحراف في الملل واختلال للدول، واشتعال للضلال واشتغال بالمحال فالعرب على عبادة الأوثان، ووأد البنات، والفرس على تعظيم النيران ووطء الأمهات والترك على تخريب البلاد، وتعذيب العباد والهند على عبادة البقر، وسجود الحجر والشجر، واليهود على الجحود والنصارى حياري فيمن ليس بوالد ولا مولود. وهكذا سائر الفرق أودية الضلال وأخبية الحيال والخبال أفيليق بحكمة الملك الحق المبين أن لا يرسل رحمة للعالمين؟ ولا يبعث من يجدد أمر الدين؟ وهل ظهر أحد يصلح لهذا الشأن، ويؤسس هذا البنيان غير محمد بن عبد الله بن عبد المطلب بن هاشم بن عبد مناف بن قصي بن كلاب بن مر بن كعب بن لؤي بن غالب بن فهر بن مالك بن النضر بن كنانة بن خزيمة بن مدركة بن إلياس بن مضر بن نزار بن معد بن عدنان عليه أفضل الصلوات وأكمل التحيات.
The fourth: that he appeared at the time when people were most in need of someone to guide to the straight path, call to the sublime religion, organize affairs, and manage the public state. Because it was a time of interregnum of messengers, divergence of paths, deviation in religions, disorder of states, prevalence of misguidance, and occupation with absurdities. The Arabs were worshipping idols and burying daughters alive; the Persians were magnifying fire and committing incest; the Turks were destroying lands and torturing servants; the Indians were worshipping cows and prostrating to stones and trees; the Jews were in denial; the Christians were bewildered about one who is neither a father nor a son. Likewise all other sects were valleys of misguidance and tents of delusion and folly. Is it befitting the wisdom of the clear true King not to send a mercy to the worlds? And not to send one who would renew the matter of religion? And has anyone appeared who is suitable for this affair and establishes this structure other than Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Abdul Muttalib ibn Hashim ibn Abd Manaf ibn Qusayy ibn Kilab ibn Murra ibn Ka'b ibn Lu'ayy ibn Ghalib ibn Fihr ibn Malik ibn al-Nadr ibn Kinana ibn Khuzayma ibn Mudrika ibn Ilyas ibn Mudar ibn Nizar ibn Ma'add ibn Adnan, upon him be the best prayers and most complete greetings?
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, which focuses on the condition of the world before the Prophet's mission. The author describes a time when all major civilizations and peoples had fallen into severe corruption and misguidance: idolatry among the Arabs, fire-worship and incest among the Persians, destruction by the Turks, idolatry in India, denial among the Jews, and confusion among the Christians about the nature of Jesus. Given this universal state of need, the author argues that it would be incompatible with God's wisdom not to send a guide. The Prophet Muhammad, with his noble lineage, is uniquely qualified for this role.

Will you actively seek the guidance He has sent through the Quran and Sunnah starting today?

The Fifth Proof: Prophecies in Scriptures
The author presents the fifth proof: prophecies of Muhammad in scriptures, and notes the deniers' objection about abrogation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim theologians used scriptural prophecies from the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms as proof of Muhammad's prophethood, and how they addressed the objection of abrogation.

Core Idea
The scriptures foretold Muhammad, and objections about abrogation stem from ignorance.

The fifth proof draws on verses from the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms that Muslim scholars interpreted as prophecies of Muhammad's coming. The author cites these verses, then identifies the real motive behind deniers' objections: not textual inaccuracy but a rejection of abrogation itself. This lesson examines these proof texts and the counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three scriptural citations
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that each citation comes from a different book: Torah, Gospel, Psalms.
These verses are treated as explicit prophecies of Muhammad.
2
Analyze the deniers' objection
The author says deniers are ignorant and stubborn, aiming to attack abrogation. Consider why abrogation is relevant.
Abrogation of previous laws is a key point in the proof.
3
Connect to broader kalam arguments
Ask yourself why scriptural prophecies are considered a rational proof in kalam, and how the objection about abrogation is dismissed.
This proof relies on the authenticity of earlier scriptures and the concept of naskh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: نصوص الكتب السماوية ففي التوراة جاء الله من طور سيناء، وأشرق من سيعير، واستعلن من جبال فاران. وفي الإنجيل، إني أطلب إلى أبي وأبيكم حتى يمنحكم ويعطيكم فارقليطا ليكون معكم إلى الأبد. وفي الزبور: تقلد أيها المختار السيف فإن ناموسك وشرائعك مقرونة بهيبة يمينك، وسهامك لمسنونة، والأمم يخرون تحتك. وأما المنكرون فأكثرهم أهل جهل وعناد وغاية متشبث الآخرين القدح في النسخ مطلقاً، وفي نسخ دين موسى خصوصاً.
The fifth: texts of the heavenly scriptures. In the Torah: 'God came from Mount Sinai, and shone from Seir, and manifested from the mountains of Paran.' In the Gospel: 'I will ask my Father and your Father to grant you and give you the Paraclete to be with you forever.' In the Psalms: 'Gird your sword, O chosen one, for your law and statutes are accompanied by the awe of your right hand, your arrows are sharpened, and nations fall beneath you.' As for the deniers, most of them are people of ignorance and stubbornness, and their ultimate aim is to criticize abrogation absolutely, and specifically the abrogation of the law of Moses.

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, citing specific verses from the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms that are interpreted as prophecies of Muhammad. The author then notes that those who deny these prophecies do so out of ignorance and obstinacy. Their real goal is to attack the concept of abrogation (naskh), especially the abrogation of the Mosaic law. The author implies that this objection is invalid and based on stubbornness.

The Fourth Proof: Universal Corruption Demands a Prophet
Al-Taftazani enumerates the corruption of various nations to prove the need for a prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the widespread corruption and moral decay in the interval between messengers proves the necessity of sending a prophet, specifically Muhammad.

Core Idea
When corruption is universal, divine guidance becomes a necessity.

Al-Taftazani presents the fourth proof for prophethood by describing the desperate state of humanity just before the advent of Muhammad. Every nation had fallen into extreme error and moral decay. This situation, he argues, demands a merciful intervention from God, and the only one who fit the role was Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Understand the historical context
Recognize that the 'interval between messengers' was a time when the world was devoid of clear guidance. Al-Taftazani sets the stage by describing this as a period of divergence and deviation.
The word 'fatra' refers to the gap between prophets.
2
Examine the corruptions of each nation
Read the list of nations—Arabs, Persians, Turks, Indians, Jews, Christians—and note their specific errors. This shows that no community was following true religion.
The passage uses vivid examples like burying daughters and worshipping fire.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical question
Al-Taftazani asks: would the All-Wise God not send a mercy to the worlds? The implied answer is no—His wisdom must send a guide. Then he concludes that Muhammad alone is that guide.
The rhetorical question is a powerful proof from divine wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه ظهر أحوج ما كان الناس إلى من يهدي إلى الطريق المستقيم، ويدعو إلى الدين العويم[؟]، وينظم الأمور، ويضبط حال الجمهور لكونه زمان فترة من الرسل، وتفرق للسبل وانحراف في الملل واختلال للدول، واشتعال للضلال واشتغال بالمحال فالعرب على عبادة الأوثان، ووأد البنات، والفرس على تعظيم النيران ووطء الأمهات والترك على تخريب البلاد، وتعذيب العباد والهند على عبادة البقر، وسجود الحجر والشجر، واليهود على الجحود والنصارى حياري فيمن ليس بوالد ولا مولود. وهكذا سائر الفرق أودية الضلال وأخبية الحيال[؟] والخبال أفيليق بحكمة الملك الحق المبين أن لا يرسل رحمة للعالمين؟ ولا يبعث من يجدد أمر الدين؟ وهل ظهر أحد يصلح لهذا الشأن، ويؤسس هذا البنيان غير محمد بن عبد الله بن عبد المطلب بن هاشم بن عبد مناف بن قصي بن كلاب بن مر بن كعب بن لؤي بن غالب بن فهر بن مالك كنانة بن خزيمة بن مدركة بن إلياس بن مضر بن نزار بن معد بن ابن النضر بن عدنان عليه أفضل الصلوات وأكمل التحيات.
The fourth: That he appeared at the time when people were most in need of someone to guide them to the straight path, call them to the right religion, organize affairs, and control the condition of the populace because it was a time of interval between messengers, divergence of ways, deviation in creeds, disorder of states, kindling of misguidance, and occupation with falsehood. The Arabs were worshipping idols and burying daughters alive; the Persians were magnifying fire and committing incest; the Turks were destroying lands and torturing servants; the Indians were worshipping cows and prostrating to stone and tree; the Jews were disbelieving; the Christians were bewildered about one who is neither father nor son. Thus all sects are valleys of misguidance and tents of illusion and insanity. Is it befitting the wisdom of the True King, the Manifest, not to send a mercy to the worlds? Not to send one who would renew the matter of religion? And did anyone appear suitable for this affair and who lays this foundation other than Muhammad ibn Abd Allah ibn Abd al-Muttalib ibn Hashim ibn Abd Manaf ibn Qusayy ibn Kilab ibn Murra ibn Ka'b ibn Lu'ayy ibn Ghalib ibn Fihr ibn Malik ibn Kinana ibn Khuzayma ibn Mudrika ibn Ilyas ibn Mudar ibn Nizar ibn Ma'd ibn ibn al-Nadr ibn Adnan, upon him be the best prayers and complete greetings.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the prophethood of Muhammad. It is a proof from the universal need for guidance at a time of widespread corruption. Al-Taftazani begins by describing the historical context: a period of interval (fatra) between messengers, when people had diverged into numerous misguided paths. He then enumerates the errors of various nations: Arabs worshipping idols and burying daughters, Persians revering fire and practicing incest, Turks destroying and torturing, Indians worshipping cows and stones, Jews in disbelief, Christians confused about the nature of Jesus. All sects are described as valleys of misguidance and tents of illusion. The author then poses a rhetorical question: would it befit the wisdom of the True King not to send a mercy to the worlds? The implied answer is no. The conclusion is that the only person who fits the role of guiding humanity and renewing religion is Muhammad, whose noble lineage is then given. The proof is based on the principle that God's wisdom requires sending guidance when people are in extreme need.

Key Terms
فترة الرسل
عبادة الأوثان
وأد البنات
أخبية الحيال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that human reason alone can achieve true guidance without the need for prophets.
Correction: The passage shows that before Muhammad, despite human reasoning, all nations fell into extreme corruption, proving that divine intervention was necessary.

Checkpoint

B) They magnified fire and committed incest.
C) They worshipped cows and prostrated to stones.

Key Takeaway
The universal corruption of all nations in the interval between messengers proves that God, in His wisdom, must send a prophet, and that prophet was Muhammad.

Scriptural Evidence from the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms
Al-Taftazani cites prophecies from earlier scriptures as proof of Muhammad's prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani uses prophecies from the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms as proofs of Muhammad's prophethood, and how he responds to the objection of abrogation.

Core Idea
The coming of Muhammad was foretold in the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms.

In this passage, Al-Taftazani presents the fifth proof for Muhammad's prophethood: explicit prophecies in the earlier revealed scriptures. He cites three verses—from the Torah, the Gospel, and the Psalms—and interprets them as referring to Muhammad. He then addresses the main objection of the deniers, which is the abrogation of previous laws.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the concise style of Al-Taftazani.
2
Identify the three scriptural citations
Find the quotations from the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms. Note what each says and how they are introduced.
The Torah citation mentions Sinai, Seir, and Paran.
3
Understand the interpretations
Reflect on how each verse is understood to refer to Muhammad. The commentary will explain the connections.
The Paraclete in the Gospel is identified with Muhammad.
4
Note the author's response to objections
Read the last part of the passage where Al-Taftazani addresses deniers. He says they are ignorant/stubborn and attack abrogation.
The final sentence affirms these texts are well-known and translated.

Source Text

الخامس : نصوص الكتب السماوية ففي التوراة جاء الله من طور سيناء، وأشرق من سيعير، واستعلن من جبال فاران. وفي الإنجيل، إني أطلب إلى أبي وأبيكم حتى يمنحكم ويعطيكم فارقليطا ليكون معكم إلى الأبد. وفي الزبور : تقلد أيها المختار السيف فإن ناموسك وشرائعك مقرونة بهيبة يمينك، وسهامك لمسنونة، والأمم يخرون تحتك . وأما المنكرون فأكثرهم أهل جهل وعناد وغاية متشبث الآخرين القدح في النسخ مطلقاً، وفي نسخ دين موسى خصوصاً. الواردة في كتب الأنبياء المتقدمين المنقولة إلى العربي، المشهورة فيما بين أممهم .
The fifth: The texts of the heavenly books. In the Torah: 'God came from Mount Sinai, shone forth from Seir, and manifested from the mountains of Paran.' And in the Gospel: 'I ask my Father and your Father until He grants you and gives you the Paraclete to be with you forever.' And in the Psalms: 'Gird yourself with the sword, O chosen one, for your law and statutes are accompanied by the awe of your right hand, and your arrows are sharpened, and the nations fall under you.' As for the deniers, most of them are people of ignorance and stubbornness, and the utmost attachment of the others is to attack abrogation absolutely, and specifically the abrogation of the law of Moses. (These texts) are transmitted in the books of the earlier prophets, translated into Arabic, and famous among their nations.

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof for Muhammad's prophethood: prophecies in earlier scriptures. Al-Taftazani quotes three verses. From the Torah: 'God came from Sinai, shone from Seir, and manifested from Paran' – interpreted as Moses (Sinai), Jesus (Seir), and Muhammad (Paran). From the Gospel: Jesus asks the Father to send the Paraclete (understood as Muhammad) to remain forever. From the Psalms: a call to the chosen one to gird his sword, with nations falling under him – seen as Muhammad. Then the author notes that deniers of these prophecies are ignorant and stubborn, and that their main objection is about abrogation (naskh) of the Mosaic law. The final sentence clarifies that these scriptural texts are well-known and transmitted in the earlier books, having been translated into Arabic.

Refutation of the First Objection: Benefit and Ruling Types
Al-Taftazani refutes first objection with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the objection that abrogation implies frivolity, ignorance, or a change of plan in God, and how the nature of rulings does not prevent abrogation.

Core Idea
Abrogation arises from newly emerging benefit, not from divine change.

The possibility of abrogation was challenged on two fronts: that it implies either frivolity or imperfection in God, and that the classifications of rulings make abrogation impossible. Al-Taftazani begins by addressing the first objection, breaking it into two sub-objections and responding to each.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow of the objection and the response. Notice the structure: 'one: ... we respond: ... second: ... we respond: ...'
2
Identify the three possibilities in the first sub-objection
The first sub-objection claims abrogation must be either frivolity, ignorance, or a change of plan. Identify each option and see why they are problematic.
The response is brief: 'for a newly arising benefit'.
3
Analyze the second sub-objection on ruling types
The second sub-objection categorizes rulings as temporary, permanent, or indefinite. Understand each category and why the objector thinks abrogation is impossible. Then study the response about indefinite rulings continuing until the time of abrogation.
The response uses the example of 'fast forever' to show conceptual eternity does not prevent abrogation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأول فلوجهين : أحدهما : أنه إما لا لمصلحة فعبث أو لمصلحة لم يعلمها أولاً فجهل، أو علمها وأهملها، ثم رعاها فبداء . قلنا : لمصلحة تجددت . وثانيهما: أن الحكم إما مؤقت، فنفيه بعده لا يكون نسخاً، وإما مؤبد، فنسخه تناقض، وإما مرسل ففي علم الله إما أن يستمر إلى الأبد فلا يرتفع، أو إلى غاية ما، فبعدها لا رفع ولا نسخ. قلنا : مرسل عن توقيت الوجوب مثلاً وتأييده وما بعده، والمعلوم استمرار الوجوب إلى وقت النسخ ولا تناقض فيه، وإن كان الواجب أبداً كما إذا قلت : صوم الأبد واجب . وأما الثاني : فلوجهين : أحدهما تواتر التأييد مثل: تمسكوا بالسبت ابداً . قلنا افتراء ولو سلم فعبارة عن طول الزمان . وثانيهما : أنه إن كان قد صرح بدوام شريعته فذاك، أو بانقطاعها لزم تواتر ذلك لنوفر الدواعي، ولم يتواتر أو سكت عن الأمرين لزم أن لا يتكرر، ولا يتقرر حتى ينسخ وقد تقرر. قلنا صرّح بالانقطاع، ولم يتواتر لقلة الدواعي والنقلة في كل طبقة، أو سحت وتقررت بحكم الأصل، أو تكرر الأسباب.
As for the first, it [may be refuted] by two faces: One: Either it is without benefit, thus frivolous, or for a benefit that He did not know initially, thus ignorance, or He knew it and neglected it then later cared for it, thus a change of plan. We respond: It was for a benefit that newly arose. Second: The ruling is either temporary, so its removal afterwards is not abrogation, or permanent, so its abrogation is a contradiction, or indefinite, then in God's knowledge it either continues forever and thus is not removed, or until a certain limit, after which there is no removal or abrogation. We respond: It is indefinite regarding the time of obligation, for example, and its confirmation and what follows; it is known that the obligation continues until the time of abrogation, and there is no contradiction in that, even if the obligation is forever, as when you say: 'Fasting forever is obligatory.' As for the second, it is refuted by two faces: One: The perpetuation is transmitted with tawatur, such as 'Hold fast to the Sabbath forever.' We respond: That is a fabrication, and even if accepted, it means a long duration. Second: If he explicitly stated the eternity of his law, that is one thing; if he explicitly stated its termination, then tawatur of that would be necessary due to the abundance of motives, but it has not been transmitted with tawatur; if he remained silent on both matters, then it follows that it would not be repeated and not established until it was abrogated, but it has been established. We respond: He explicitly stated the termination, and it was not transmitted with tawatur due to the lack of motives and transmitters in each generation, or it was falsehood and it was established by the original ruling, or the causes were repeated.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage refutes two objections against the abrogation of previous scriptures, especially the Mosaic law. The first objection has two parts. (a) If there is no benefit, abrogation is frivolous; if God did not know the benefit initially, it implies ignorance; if He knew but neglected then later cared, it implies a change of plan (bada'). The response: abrogation occurs for a newly arising benefit, not due to ignorance or change. (b) A ruling is either temporary (so removal is not abrogation), permanent (so abrogation is contradiction), or indefinite (so either it continues forever or ends at a limit). The response: the ruling is indefinite regarding the duration of obligation; it continues until the time of abrogation, and there is no contradiction even if the obligation is conceptually eternal (like 'fast forever') because the obligation is conditional on the period before abrogation. The second objection also has two parts. (a) Some previous commands (e.g., Sabbath) were commanded forever, implying abrogation is impossible. The response: that claim is a fabrication; even if accepted, 'forever' means a long duration, not literal eternity. (b) If a prophet explicitly stated the permanence of his law, that is final; if he explicitly stated its termination, that statement must be mutawatir (massively transmitted), but it is not; if he was silent, the law cannot be established and then abrogated. The response: the prophet did state termination, but it was not transmitted via tawatur due to lack of motives and few transmitters in each generation; or the claim of perpetuity is false and the abrogation is established by the original principle (e.g., the oneness of God); or the causes for abrogation recurred. The argument defends the consistency of abrogation with divine wisdom and revelation.

Key Terms
النسخ
المصلحة
البداء
التوقيت
التأييد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation means God changes His mind or was ignorant of what is best.
Correction: Abrogation occurs for a newly arising benefit, not due to ignorance or change; God's knowledge and wisdom remain perfect.

Checkpoint
According to the first sub-objection, what three possibilities does abrogation supposedly imply?
A) Frivolity, ignorance, or change of plan
B) Temporary, permanent, or indefinite rulings
C) New benefit, old benefit, or no benefit
D) Contradiction, repetition, or silence

Key Takeaway
Abrogation is justified by a newly arising benefit and does not contradict the nature of rulings, as indefinite rulings continue until the divinely appointed time of abrogation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a seeming contradiction in the Quran or Sunnah (like different rulings), what is your default assumption about Allah’s wisdom? Do you immediately doubt His knowledge, or do you seek the underlying benefit that may be newly arising? Will you commit to investigating such issues with this principle?
?
The objection includes the idea that abrogation would be a 'change of plan' (badā'). Have you ever harbored the subtle belief that God changes His mind like a human? How does affirming that abrogation is for a newly arising benefit reshape your trust in His immutability and wisdom? What step will you take to uproot any anthropomorphic thoughts?
?
If a ruling is expressed as 'forever' in revelation (e.g., fasting forever), would you struggle to accept its abrogation? This lesson shows that such expressions are conditional. How does this affect your understanding of some amr (commands) that appear absolute? Will you humble yourself before Allah’s knowledge, which knows the true endpoint even when it is not stated?

Refutation of the Second Objection: Perpetual Commands and Transmission
Al-Taftazani refutes second objection with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the objection that abrogation is impossible because previous laws were commanded forever, using arguments about transmission and interpretation.

Core Idea
Claims of perpetual commandments are either fabricated or mean a long duration, not literal eternity.

The second objection against abrogation relies on scriptural commands that seem perpetual, like the Sabbath, and on the implications of prophetic statements. Al-Taftazani responds by questioning the reliability of transmission and the literal interpretation of 'eternity'.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage to see the two sub-objections and the responses. Note the use of terms like تواتر (tawatur) and تأييد (perpetuation).
2
Understand the first sub-objection about tawatur
The objector claims that commands like 'Hold fast to the Sabbath forever' are mutawatir (massively transmitted). Identify the response: it is a fabrication, or 'forever' means a long duration.
Tawatur is a key concept in hadith criticism; the response challenges both the authenticity and the meaning.
3
Analyze the second sub-objection on prophetic statements
The objector presents three scenarios: prophet explicitly states permanence, explicitly states termination, or remains silent. Understand the objection's logic and the response that termination was stated but not mutawatir due to lack of motives, or is established by other means.
The response offers multiple possibilities to show the objection does not hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأول فلوجهين : أحدهما : أنه إما لا لمصلحة فعبث أو لمصلحة لم يعلمها أولاً فجهل، أو علمها وأهملها، ثم رعاها فبداء . قلنا : لمصلحة تجددت . وثانيهما: أن الحكم إما مؤقت، فنفيه بعده لا يكون نسخاً، وإما مؤبد، فنسخه تناقض، وإما مرسل ففي علم الله إما أن يستمر إلى الأبد فلا يرتفع، أو إلى غاية ما، فبعدها لا رفع ولا نسخ. قلنا : مرسل عن توقيت الوجوب مثلاً وتأييده وما بعده، والمعلوم استمرار الوجوب إلى وقت النسخ ولا تناقض فيه، وإن كان الواجب أبداً كما إذا قلت : صوم الأبد واجب . وأما الثاني : فلوجهين : أحدهما تواتر التأييد مثل: تمسكوا بالسبت ابداً . قلنا افتراء ولو سلم فعبارة عن طول الزمان . وثانيهما : أنه إن كان قد صرح بدوام شريعته فذاك، أو بانقطاعها لزم تواتر ذلك لنوفر الدواعي، ولم يتواتر أو سكت عن الأمرين لزم أن لا يتكرر، ولا يتقرر حتى ينسخ وقد تقرر. قلنا صرّح بالانقطاع، ولم يتواتر لقلة الدواعي والنقلة في كل طبقة، أو سحت وتقررت بحكم الأصل، أو تكرر الأسباب.
As for the first, it [may be refuted] by two faces: One: Either it is without benefit, thus frivolous, or for a benefit that He did not know initially, thus ignorance, or He knew it and neglected it then later cared for it, thus a change of plan. We respond: It was for a benefit that newly arose. Second: The ruling is either temporary, so its removal afterwards is not abrogation, or permanent, so its abrogation is a contradiction, or indefinite, then in God's knowledge it either continues forever and thus is not removed, or until a certain limit, after which there is no removal or abrogation. We respond: It is indefinite regarding the time of obligation, for example, and its confirmation and what follows; it is known that the obligation continues until the time of abrogation, and there is no contradiction in that, even if the obligation is forever, as when you say: 'Fasting forever is obligatory.' As for the second, it is refuted by two faces: One: The perpetuation is transmitted with tawatur, such as 'Hold fast to the Sabbath forever.' We respond: That is a fabrication, and even if accepted, it means a long duration. Second: If he explicitly stated the eternity of his law, that is one thing; if he explicitly stated its termination, then tawatur of that would be necessary due to the abundance of motives, but it has not been transmitted with tawatur; if he remained silent on both matters, then it follows that it would not be repeated and not established until it was abrogated, but it has been established. We respond: He explicitly stated the termination, and it was not transmitted with tawatur due to the lack of motives and transmitters in each generation, or it was falsehood and it was established by the original ruling, or the causes were repeated.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage refutes two objections against the abrogation of previous scriptures, especially the Mosaic law. The first objection has two parts. (a) If there is no benefit, abrogation is frivolous; if God did not know the benefit initially, it implies ignorance; if He knew but neglected then later cared, it implies a change of plan (bada'). The response: abrogation occurs for a newly arising benefit, not due to ignorance or change. (b) A ruling is either temporary (so removal is not abrogation), permanent (so abrogation is contradiction), or indefinite (so either it continues forever or ends at a limit). The response: the ruling is indefinite regarding the duration of obligation; it continues until the time of abrogation, and there is no contradiction even if the obligation is conceptually eternal (like 'fast forever') because the obligation is conditional on the period before abrogation. The second objection also has two parts. (a) Some previous commands (e.g., Sabbath) were commanded forever, implying abrogation is impossible. The response: that claim is a fabrication; even if accepted, 'forever' means a long duration, not literal eternity. (b) If a prophet explicitly stated the permanence of his law, that is final; if he explicitly stated its termination, that statement must be mutawatir (massively transmitted), but it is not; if he was silent, the law cannot be established and then abrogated. The response: the prophet did state termination, but it was not transmitted via tawatur due to lack of motives and few transmitters in each generation; or the claim of perpetuity is false and the abrogation is established by the original principle (e.g., the oneness of God); or the causes for abrogation recurred. The argument defends the consistency of abrogation with divine wisdom and revelation.

Key Terms
التواتر
التأييد
النسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation contradicts scripture that commands something forever, like the Sabbath.
Correction: Scriptural claims of perpetuity are either not authentic or use 'forever' to mean a long duration; thus abrogation remains possible.

Checkpoint
According to the first sub-objection of the second objection, what evidence is presented for the impossibility of abrogation?
A) A rational argument about the nature of rulings
B) A mutawatir report commanding perpetual observance
C) The silence of the prophet on the duration of his law
D) The lack of benefit in abrogation

Key Takeaway
Alleged perpetual commands are either fabrications or use 'forever' figuratively, and even if a prophet stated termination, lack of tawatur does not invalidate abrogation due to contextual factors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious claim that 'this has always been the rule' or 'this is eternal,' do you accept it at face value, or do you test it against authenticity (like al-Taftazani tests the Sabbath claim)? What personal experiences have made you either cautious or gullible about such claims? Today, commit to verifying one commonly-held 'eternal' teaching in your own tradition with scholarly sources.
?
The passage mentions that a prophet's explicit statement of termination was not widely transmitted due to 'lack of motives and transmitters.' How many sacred truths in your own life might have been lost or obscured simply because people lacked the motivation to preserve them? Ask yourself: what responsibility do you bear to transmit the truths you have received, especially if they are difficult or counter-cultural?
?
Al-Taftazani counters that an objection relies on an unrealistic expectation of tawatur. Are there areas in your faith where you demand overwhelming proof for something that, by its nature, could not be transmitted en masse? Could your demand for 'certainty' be a subtle excuse not to submit? Repent of any intellectual pride that prevents you from accepting what has been reliably though not massively transmitted.

Refuting Two Objections to Abrogation
Author refutes two objections against abrogation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes two key objections against abrogation, showing it does not imply ignorance, contradiction, or change in God's knowledge.

Core Idea
Abrogation reflects newly arising benefit, not change in God's knowledge.

This passage tackles two major objections to the possibility of abrogation (naskh) in divine law. Critics argue that abrogation implies either frivolity, ignorance, or a change of mind in God (badā'), or that it leads to contradictions in eternal rulings. The author systematically refutes each objection, clarifying that God's knowledge is fixed and that rulings can be specified to a duration without inconsistency. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Follow the structure of objections and replies
Notice that the author presents each objection with 'As for the first...' and 'As for the second...', each with two aspects. Read along and mark the structure.
The responses begin with 'We say' (قلنا).
2
Analyze the first objection's two aspects
The first objection claims abrogation is either frivolous (no benefit), based on ignorance, or a change of mind (badā'). Understand each charge.
The objection tries to show that any possible scenario for abrogation is problematic.
3
Study the author's first reply
He answers simply: the benefit newly arose. This shows the ruling was not initially without benefit, but a new benefit arose later. The second aspect is handled by clarifying that unspecified rulings continue until the time of abrogation.
This preserves God's knowledge and purpose.
4
Analyze the second objection's two aspects
The second objection involves two claims: (a) there is widespread transmission (tawātur) that previous laws are eternal, like the Sabbath; (b) if God explicitly stated perpetuity or cessation, or remained silent, each leads to a problem.
The objection seeks to prove that abrogation cannot occur if a law was meant to last forever.
5
Study the author's second reply
He refutes the tawātur claim as false or metaphorical. For the second aspect, he states that God explicitly stated cessation, but the transmission was scarce due to few motives and transmitters, or the law was established by the original ruling.
Thus, abrogation is still possible without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اما الأول فلوجهين أحدهما : أنه إما لا لمصلحة فعبث أو لمصلحة لم يعلمها أولاً فجهل، أو علمها وأهملها، ثم رعاها فبداء . قلنا : لمصلحة تجددت . وثانيهما: أن الحكم إما مؤقت، فنفيه بعده لا يكون نسخاً، وإما مؤبد، فنسخه تناقض، وإما مرسل ففي علم الله إما أن يستمر إلى الأبد فلا يرتفع، أو إلى غاية ما، فبعدها لا رفع ولا نسخ. قلنا : مرسل عن توقيت الوجوب مثلاً وتأييده وما بعده، والمعلوم استمرار الوجوب إلى وقت النسخ ولا تناقض فيه، وإن كان الواجب أبداً كما إذا قلت : صوم الأبد واجب . وأما الثاني : فلوجهين : أحدهما تواتر التأييد مثل: تمسكوا بالسبت ابداً . قلنا افتراء ولو سلم فعبارة عن طول الزمان . وثانيهما : أنه إن كان قد صرح بدوام شريعته فذاك، أو بانقطاعها لزم تواتر ذلك لنوفر الدواعي، ولم يتواتر أو سكت عن الأمرين لزم أن لا يتكرر، ولا يتقرر حتى ينسخ وقد تقرر. قلنا صرّح بالانقطاع، ولم يتواتر لقلة الدواعي والنقلة في كل طبقة، أو سحت وتقررت بحكم الأصل، أو تكرر الأسباب.
As for the first [objection], it is from two aspects: one of them: [the abrogation] is either without benefit, so it is frivolous; or it is for a benefit He did not know initially, so it is ignorance; or He knew it and neglected it, then observed it, so it is a change of mind (badā'). We say: It is for a benefit that newly arose. And the second aspect: the ruling is either temporary, so its negation after [the time] is not abrogation; or eternal, so its abrogation is a contradiction; or unspecified, so in God's knowledge it either continues forever and thus is not removed, or [it continues] up to a certain limit, after which there is no removal or abrogation. We say: [It is] unspecified regarding the timing of obligation, for example, and its perpetuation and what follows, and what is known is the continuation of the obligation until the time of abrogation, and there is no contradiction in that, even if the obligation is forever, as when you say: 'Fasting forever is obligatory.' As for the second [objection], it is from two aspects: one of them: the widespread transmission (tawātur) of perpetuity, such as 'Hold fast to the Sabbath forever.' We say: It is a fabrication, and even if it is accepted, it is an expression for a long time. And the second aspect: if He explicitly stated the perpetuity of His law, then that is [settled]; or if He stated its cessation, then its widespread transmission would be necessary due to the abundance of motives, yet it is not transmitted widely; or if He remained silent about both matters, then it would not be established or confirmed until abrogated, but it has been confirmed. We say: He explicitly stated the cessation, and it was not widely transmitted due to the scarcity of motives and transmitters at each level, or it was established by the original ruling, or the reasons recurred.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two objections against the occurrence of abrogation (naskh) in divine law. The author presents each objection followed by his answer. The first objection has two aspects: either the abrogation is without benefit (thus frivolous), or it is for a benefit unknown to God initially (thus ignorance), or known but neglected then later enacted (thus a change of mind, badā'). The author answers that the benefit newly arose. The second aspect of the first objection: the ruling is either temporary (so negating it later is not abrogation), eternal (so abrogation would be a contradiction), or unspecified (so in God's knowledge it either continues forever or ends at a limit, in which case no removal occurs). The author answers that the unspecified ruling is concerning the timing of obligation and its perpetuation; it is known that the obligation continues until the time of abrogation, with no contradiction. The second objection also has two aspects. First: there is claimed wide transmission (tawātur) of perpetuity, as in 'Hold fast to the Sabbath forever.' The author answers that this is a fabrication, or if accepted, it means a long duration. Second: if God explicitly stated perpetuity, it is settled; if He stated cessation, wide transmission would be necessary due to abundant incentives, but it is not transmitted; if He was silent, the law would not be established until abrogated, yet it has been established. The author answers that God explicitly stated cessation, but it was not widely transmitted due to few motives and transmitters at each level, or it was established by the original ruling, or the reasons recurred.

Key Terms
نسخ (naskh)
بداء (badā')
تواتر (tawātur)
مؤقت (muwaqqat)
مؤبد (mu'abbad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation implies God was ignorant or changed His mind.
Correction: The author shows that abrogation occurs for a newly arising benefit, not due to ignorance or change in God's knowledge; the duration of rulings is fixed in God's knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the answer to the objection that abrogation would be either frivolous or based on ignorance?
A) Abrogation is a change in God's will.
B) The benefit of the ruling newly arose.
C) The ruling was never intended to be permanent.
D) There is no benefit in abrogation.

Key Takeaway
Abrogation in divine law is rationally defensible: it occurs when a new benefit arises, and God's knowledge of the ruling's duration is fixed, without contradiction or change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted the consistency of God's commands because a ruling seems to change? How does this lesson's explanation of abrogation as based on newly arising benefit challenge your doubts? What specific area of your faith do you need to trust more deeply because of this?
?
When you find it hard to obey a divine command, do you ever secretly wish it were abrogated or updated? How does the author's defense of abrogation—that it is for a genuine benefit—force you to reconsider your attitude toward obedience? What will you commit to changing in your heart about that command?
?
The objection that abrogation implies ignorance or change in God is refuted by affirming His fixed knowledge. Where in your life do you act as if God's guidance is temporary or subject to your own changing feelings? How can you align your commitment to a ruling even when its benefit is not immediately apparent?

Torah Prophecies About the Prophet Muhammad
The author cites three Torah prophecies and interprets them as predictions of Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three Torah prophecies that the author interprets as foretelling the coming of the Prophet Muhammad.

Core Idea
The Torah foretells the Prophet Muhammad as a prophet from the brethren of the Israelites.

The author presents several passages from the Torah that he argues point to the prophethood of Muhammad. These passages, found in Deuteronomy and Genesis, are interpreted in light of geography and lineage to show that Muhammad, an Ishmaelite, is the promised prophet like Moses. Let's examine these prophecies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Torah passages
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the three distinct prophecies cited from the Torah.
Notice how the author identifies locations and lineages.
2
Identify each prophecy
Identify the three prophecies: (1) God's coming from Sinai, Seir, and Paran, (2) a prophet raised from the brethren of the Israelites, (3) Hagar's descendant whose hand is above all.
Each prophecy is from a different book of the Torah.
3
Analyze the interpretation
Understand how the author interprets each prophecy to refer specifically to Muhammad, using geographical and genealogical arguments.
The author uses the location of Paran and the lineage of Ishmael.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواردة في كتب الأنبياء المتقدمين المنقولة إلى العربي، المشهورة فيما بين أممهم . أما من التوراة فمنها ما جاء في السفر الخامس : جاء الله من طور سيناء، وأشرق من سيعير، واستعلن من جبال فاران ، يريد الإخبار عن إنزال التوراة على موسى بطور سيناء والإنجيل على عيسى بسيعير، فإنه كان يسكن من سيعير بقرية تسمى ناصرة، وأنزل القرآن على محمد بمكة . فإن فاران في طريق مكة قبل العدن بميلين ونصف وهو كان المنزل، وقد بقي إلى اليوم على يسار الطريق من العراق إلى مكة. وهذا ما ذكر في التوراة أن إسماعيل أقام برية فاران ، يعني بادية العرب. ومنها ما جاء في السفر الخامس أنه تعالى قال لموسى : إني مقيم لهم نبياً من بني إخوتهم مثلك ، وأجري قولي في فيه ويقول لهم ما آمرهم به، والرجل الذي لا يقبل قول النبي الذي يتكلم باسمي فأنا انتقم منه . والمراد ببني أخوة بني إسرائيل بنو إسماعيل على ما هو المتعارف، فلا يصرف إلى من بعد موسى من أنبياء بني إسرائيل، ولا إلى عيسى لأنهم لم يكونوا من بني إخوتهم، ولا إلى موسى لكونه صاحب شريعة مستأنفة فيها بيان مصالح الدارين، فتعين محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ومنها ما جاء في السفر الأول أنه تعالى قال لإبراهيم : إن هاجر تلد، ويكون من ولدها من يده فوق الجميع ، ويد الجميع مبسوطة إليه بالخشوع . وأما في الإنجيل، فمنها ما ورد في الصحاح الرابع عشر: أنا أطلب لكم إلى أبي حتى يمنحكم ويعطيكم فارقليطا ليكون معكم إلى الأبد. والفارقليط روح الحق واليقين. وفي الخامس عشر : وأما فارقليط روح القدس الذي يرسله أبي باسمي، وهو يعلمكم ويمنحكم جميع الاشياء، وهو يذكركم ما قلته لكم، ثم قال: وإني قد أخبرتكم بهذا قبل أن يكون حتى إذا كان ذلك تؤمنوا به. وقوله: «باسمي» يعني
That which is found in the books of the earlier prophets transmitted into Arabic, well-known among their nations. As for from the Torah, among it is what came in the fifth book: 'God came from Mount Sinai, and shone from Seir, and appeared from the mountains of Paran' – meaning to inform about the sending down of the Torah upon Moses at Mount Sinai, and the Gospel upon Jesus at Seir, for he was dwelling in Seir in a village called Nazareth, and the sending down of the Quran upon Muhammad in Mecca. Indeed, Paran is on the road to Mecca two and a half miles before 'Adan, and it was the descent, and it remains to this day on the left side of the road from Iraq to Mecca. This is what is mentioned in the Torah that Ishmael settled in the wilderness of Paran, meaning the desert of the Arabs. And among it is what came in the fifth book that He, exalted, said to Moses: 'I will raise up for them a prophet from among their brethren like you, and I will put My words in his mouth, and he will speak to them all that I command him. And the man who does not accept the word of the prophet who speaks in My name, I will take vengeance on him.' The intended meaning of 'the brethren of the Children of Israel' is the Children of Ishmael, according to what is customary, so it cannot be redirected to the prophets of the Children of Israel after Moses, nor to Jesus because they were not from their brethren, nor to Moses because he was the possessor of a new law containing the explanation of the benefits of the two abodes. Thus it is specifically Muhammad, peace be upon him. And among it is what came in the first book that He, exalted, said to Abraham: 'Indeed, Hagar will give birth, and from her child there will be one whose hand is above all, and the hand of all is extended to him in humility.' As for the Gospel, among it is what is reported in the fourteenth chapter: 'I will ask the Father for you until He grants you and gives you a Paraclete to be with you forever.' And the Paraclete is the spirit of truth and certainty. And in the fifteenth: 'But the Paraclete, the Holy Spirit, whom the Father will send in My name, He will teach you and grant you all things, and He will remind you of what I have said to you.' Then He said: 'And I have told you this before it happens, so that when it does happen, you may believe in it.' And His saying: 'in My name' means
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents prophecies from the Torah and Gospel that the author interprets as foretelling the prophethood of Muhammad. It begins by stating that these prophecies are found in the earlier scriptures transmitted into Arabic and well-known among the nations. Three examples are given from the Torah. The first is from Deuteronomy (the fifth book): 'God came from Sinai, shone from Seir, and appeared from Paran.' The author interprets this as referring to the revelation of the Torah to Moses at Sinai, the Gospel to Jesus at Seir (Nazareth), and the Quran to Muhammad at Mecca, identifying Paran as a location near Mecca where Ishmael settled. The second prophecy: God tells Moses He will raise up a prophet from among the brethren of the Israelites (interpreted as the Ishmaelites, i.e., Arabs), like Moses, putting words in his mouth. The author argues this cannot refer to any later Israelite prophet, nor to Jesus (since they are not their brethren), nor to Moses himself, so it must be Muhammad. The third prophecy from Genesis (first book): God tells Abraham that Hagar will bear a child whose hand will be above all, implying Ishmael's descendants' prominence. Then from the Gospel, the author cites passages from John 14 and 15 about the Paraclete (Comforter). The Paraclete is described as the Spirit of Truth who will be with them forever, teach them, and remind them. The author interprets this as referring to Muhammad. The passage ends abruptly with 'meaning' (ya'nī), likely continuing the interpretation.

Key Terms
Torah (توراة)
Ishmael (إسماعيل)
brethren (إخوة)
Paran (فاران)
Seir (سيعير)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The prophecy 'I will raise up a prophet from among their brethren' refers to a later Israelite prophet like Jeremiah or Ezekiel.
Correction: The author argues it cannot refer to later Israelite prophets because they are from the same people, not from their brethren; it refers to the Ishmaelites (Arabs), specifically Muhammad.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the significance of Paran in the first prophecy?
A) It is the place where Moses received the Torah.
B) It is a mountain near Seir.
C) It is the location where Muhammad received the Quran, near Mecca.
D) It is the wilderness where Jesus lived.

Key Takeaway
Three Torah passages are interpreted as clear prophecies of Muhammad's coming, using geographical and genealogical arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the fact that the Torah—a scripture you believe was revealed by God—explicitly foretells the coming of Muhammad change the way you view your own relationship with that scripture? Are you studying the previous revelations with the same reverence as the Quran, or have you neglected them?
?
If God planned from the time of Abraham and Moses to send Muhammad as a prophet, what does that say about the urgency of following his message today? What would it mean for your life if you truly believed that your salvation depends on accepting this prophecy?
?
The author uses geography and genealogy as proofs. How strong is your own conviction that Muhammad is a prophet? Have you ever examined the prophecies about him in previous scriptures for yourself, or are you relying on others' claims? What step will you take this week to personally verify this evidence?

Gospel Prophecies About the Prophet Muhammad: The Paraclete
The author cites two passages from John about the Paraclete and interprets them as prophecies of Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author interprets the Gospel passages about the Paraclete as referring to the Prophet Muhammad.

Core Idea
The Paraclete promised in the Gospel is interpreted as a future prophet, not the Holy Spirit.

In the Gospel of John, Jesus promises a Paraclete (Comforter) who will teach and remind. While Christians understand this as the Holy Spirit, the author argues it refers to a human prophet, namely Muhammad. Let's analyze these verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the Gospel passages
Read the Arabic passage and translation from the Gospel of John. Focus on the description of the Paraclete.
The Paraclete is also called the Spirit of Truth.
2
Understand the author's interpretation
Note how the author views the Paraclete as a prophet who will teach and remind, and connects 'in My name' to Muhammad.
The passage ends abruptly; the interpretation is cut off.
3
Compare with Christian understanding
Reflect on the difference: Christians see the Paraclete as the Holy Spirit, while the author sees a human prophet.
Consider the implications for interfaith dialogue.

الواردة في كتب الأنبياء المتقدمين المنقولة إلى العربي، المشهورة فيما بين أممهم . أما من التوراة فمنها ما جاء في السفر الخامس : جاء الله من طور سيناء، وأشرق من سيعير، واستعلن من جبال فاران ، يريد الإخبار عن إنزال التوراة على موسى بطور سيناء والإنجيل على عيسى بسيعير، فإنه كان يسكن من سيعير بقرية تسمى ناصرة، وأنزل القرآن على محمد بمكة . فإن فاران في طريق مكة قبل العدن بميلين ونصف وهو كان المنزل، وقد بقي إلى اليوم على يسار الطريق من العراق إلى مكة. وهذا ما ذكر في التوراة أن إسماعيل أقام برية فاران ، يعني بادية العرب. ومنها ما جاء في السفر الخامس أنه تعالى قال لموسى : إني مقيم لهم نبياً من بني إخوتهم مثلك ، وأجري قولي في فيه ويقول لهم ما آمرهم به، والرجل الذي لا يقبل قول النبي الذي يتكلم باسمي فأنا انتقم منه . والمراد ببني أخوة بني إسرائيل بنو إسماعيل على ما هو المتعارف، فلا يصرف إلى من بعد موسى من أنبياء بني إسرائيل، ولا إلى عيسى لأنهم لم يكونوا من بني إخوتهم، ولا إلى موسى لكونه صاحب شريعة مستأنفة فيها بيان مصالح الدارين، فتعين محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ومنها ما جاء في السفر الأول أنه تعالى قال لإبراهيم : إن هاجر تلد، ويكون من ولدها من يده فوق الجميع ، ويد الجميع مبسوطة إليه بالخشوع . وأما في الإنجيل، فمنها ما ورد في الصحاح الرابع عشر: أنا أطلب لكم إلى أبي حتى يمنحكم ويعطيكم فارقليطا ليكون معكم إلى الأبد. والفارقليط روح الحق واليقين. وفي الخامس عشر : وأما فارقليط روح القدس الذي يرسله أبي باسمي، وهو يعلمكم ويمنحكم جميع الاشياء، وهو يذكركم ما قلته لكم، ثم قال: وإني قد أخبرتكم بهذا قبل أن يكون حتى إذا كان ذلك تؤمنوا به. وقوله: «باسمي» يعني
That which is found in the books of the earlier prophets transmitted into Arabic, well-known among their nations. As for from the Torah, among it is what came in the fifth book: 'God came from Mount Sinai, and shone from Seir, and appeared from the mountains of Paran' – meaning to inform about the sending down of the Torah upon Moses at Mount Sinai, and the Gospel upon Jesus at Seir, for he was dwelling in Seir in a village called Nazareth, and the sending down of the Quran upon Muhammad in Mecca. Indeed, Paran is on the road to Mecca two and a half miles before 'Adan, and it was the descent, and it remains to this day on the left side of the road from Iraq to Mecca. This is what is mentioned in the Torah that Ishmael settled in the wilderness of Paran, meaning the desert of the Arabs. And among it is what came in the fifth book that He, exalted, said to Moses: 'I will raise up for them a prophet from among their brethren like you, and I will put My words in his mouth, and he will speak to them all that I command him. And the man who does not accept the word of the prophet who speaks in My name, I will take vengeance on him.' The intended meaning of 'the brethren of the Children of Israel' is the Children of Ishmael, according to what is customary, so it cannot be redirected to the prophets of the Children of Israel after Moses, nor to Jesus because they were not from their brethren, nor to Moses because he was the possessor of a new law containing the explanation of the benefits of the two abodes. Thus it is specifically Muhammad, peace be upon him. And among it is what came in the first book that He, exalted, said to Abraham: 'Indeed, Hagar will give birth, and from her child there will be one whose hand is above all, and the hand of all is extended to him in humility.' As for the Gospel, among it is what is reported in the fourteenth chapter: 'I will ask the Father for you until He grants you and gives you a Paraclete to be with you forever.' And the Paraclete is the spirit of truth and certainty. And in the fifteenth: 'But the Paraclete, the Holy Spirit, whom the Father will send in My name, He will teach you and grant you all things, and He will remind you of what I have said to you.' Then He said: 'And I have told you this before it happens, so that when it does happen, you may believe in it.' And His saying: 'in My name' means
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents prophecies from the Torah and Gospel that the author interprets as foretelling the prophethood of Muhammad. It begins by stating that these prophecies are found in the earlier scriptures transmitted into Arabic and well-known among the nations. Three examples are given from the Torah. The first is from Deuteronomy (the fifth book): 'God came from Sinai, shone from Seir, and appeared from Paran.' The author interprets this as referring to the revelation of the Torah to Moses at Sinai, the Gospel to Jesus at Seir (Nazareth), and the Quran to Muhammad at Mecca, identifying Paran as a location near Mecca where Ishmael settled. The second prophecy: God tells Moses He will raise up a prophet from among the brethren of the Israelites (interpreted as the Ishmaelites, i.e., Arabs), like Moses, putting words in his mouth. The author argues this cannot refer to any later Israelite prophet, nor to Jesus (since they are not their brethren), nor to Moses himself, so it must be Muhammad. The third prophecy from Genesis (first book): God tells Abraham that Hagar will bear a child whose hand will be above all, implying Ishmael's descendants' prominence. Then from the Gospel, the author cites passages from John 14 and 15 about the Paraclete (Comforter). The Paraclete is described as the Spirit of Truth who will be with them forever, teach them, and remind them. The author interprets this as referring to Muhammad. The passage ends abruptly with 'meaning' (ya'nī), likely continuing the interpretation.

Key Terms
Paraclete (فارقليط)
Gospel (إنجيل)
Holy Spirit (روح القدس)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Paraclete is the Holy Spirit, the third person of the Trinity.
Correction: The author argues it is a human prophet, Muhammad, based on the description of teaching and reminding, and the phrase 'in My name' meaning like Jesus.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the role of the Paraclete?
A) To be a divine spirit that permanently indwells believers.
B) To teach and remind believers of Jesus' teachings.
C) To be a prophet who will bring a new law.
D) To intercede for believers before God.

Key Takeaway
The Gospel passages about the Paraclete are interpreted as prophecies of Muhammad, not the Holy Spirit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Paraclete is described as the one who 'will remind you of what I have said to you.' The Quran is that reminder for us. When did you last let the Quran disturb your comfort and change a habit? What will you do today to truly listen to its reminder?
?
Jesus promised a guide who would teach all truth. You believe that guide was Muhammad (peace be upon him). Is there any area of your life where you still resist his teachings? Name one and commit to a small step of obedience this week.
?
The author interprets 'in My name' to mean 'like me'. In what specific character trait do you need to become more like the Prophet? Write down one concrete action you will take tomorrow to embody that trait.

The Sinai-Seir-Paran Prophecy
First Torah prophecy interpreted as three revelations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a Torah verse about God's appearance at Sinai, Seir, and Paran is interpreted to foretell the revelations of Torah, Gospel, and Quran, and how this points to Muhammad.

Core Idea
The Torah itself points to three revelations: Sinai, Seir, and Paran.

The author begins with a verse from Deuteronomy that describes God's appearance from three locations. He interprets these as the giving of the Torah to Moses, the Gospel to Jesus, and the Quran to Muhammad. This lesson unpacks that interpretation and its geographical grounding in identifying Paran with Mecca.

Guided Path
1
Read the first Torah evidence
Read the Arabic and translation of the first prophecy carefully. Notice the three locations mentioned and the author's interpretation.
This verse is from Deuteronomy 33:2.
2
Understand the geographical identification
The author identifies Paran as a place near Mecca on the road from Iraq. Relate this to the settlement of Ishmael and the connection to Muhammad.
Paran is identified as a wilderness where Ishmael lived.
3
Reflect on the logical chain
Consider how the verse's structure—Sinai (Moses), Seir (Jesus), Paran (Muhammad)—supports the claim of successive revelations culminating in Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما من التوراة فمنها ما جاء في السفر الخامس جاء الله من طور سيناء، وأشرق من سيعير، واستعلن من جبال فاران، يريد الإخبار عن إنزال التوراة على موسى بطور سيناء والإنجيل على عيسى بسيعير، فإنه كان يسكن من سيعير بقرية تسمى ناصرة، وأنزل القرآن على محمد بمكة. فإن فاران في طريق مكة قبل العدن بميلين ونصف وهو كان المنزل، وقد بقي إلى اليوم على يسار الطريق من العراق إلى مكة. وهذا ما ذكر في التوراة أن إسماعيل أقام برية فاران، يعني بادية العرب. ومنها ما جاء في السفر الخامس أنه تعالى قال لموسى: إني مقيم لهم نبياً من بني إخوتهم مثلك، وأجري قولي في فيه ويقول لهم ما آمرهم به، والرجل الذي لا يقبل قول النبي الذي يتكلم باسمي فأنا انتقم منه. والمراد ببني أخوة بني إسرائيل بنو إسماعيل على ما هو المتعارف، فلا يصرف إلى من بعد موسى من أنبياء بني إسرائيل، ولا إلى عيسى لأنهم لم يكونوا من بني إخوتهم، ولا إلى موسى لكونه صاحب شريعة مستأنفة فيها بيان مصالح الدارين، فتعين محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم. ومنها ما جاء في السفر الأول أنه تعالى قال لإبراهيم (عليه السلام): إن هاجر تلد، ويكون من ولدها من يده فوق الجميع، ويد الجميع مبسوطة إليه بالخشوع.
As for from the Torah, among them is what came in the fifth book: 'God came from Mount Sinai, and shone forth from Seir, and appeared from the mountains of Paran' – meaning to inform of the revelation of the Torah to Moses at Mount Sinai, and the Gospel to Jesus at Seir, for he was dwelling in Seir in a village called Nazareth, and the Quran was revealed to Muhammad in Mecca. For Paran is on the road to Mecca, two and a half miles before al-‘Adan, and it was the stopping place, and it has remained to this day on the left side of the road from Iraq to Mecca. And this is what is mentioned in the Torah, that Ishmael settled in the wilderness of Paran, meaning the Arabian desert. And among what came in the fifth book, that God said to Moses: 'I will raise up for them a prophet from among their brethren like you, and I will put my words in his mouth, and he will say to them all that I command him. And the man who does not listen to the words of the prophet who speaks in my name, I will take vengeance on him.' And the intended meaning of 'brethren of the children of Israel' is the children of Ishmael, according to what is customary, so it cannot be turned to the prophets after Moses from among the children of Israel, nor to Jesus, because they were not from among their brethren, nor to Moses, because he was the possessor of a new law containing the explanation of the interests of both abodes. Thus, it is determined to be Muhammad, peace be upon him. And among what came in the first book, that God said to Abraham (peace be upon him): 'Indeed Hagar will give birth, and from her offspring will be one whose hand is above all, and the hands of all are stretched out to him in humility.'
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents three pieces of evidence from the Torah for the prophethood of Muhammad. First, the verse from Deuteronomy 33:2 is interpreted as referring to the revelations at Sinai (Torah), Seir (Gospel), and Paran (Quran). Paran is identified as a place on the road from Iraq to Mecca where Ishmael settled. Second, Deuteronomy 18:18 promises a prophet from the brethren of Israel, which the author explains are the Ishmaelites, not later Israelite prophets or Jesus, thereby identifying Muhammad. Third, Genesis 16:11-12 mentions Ishmael's descendants having the upper hand, which is fulfilled in Muhammad. The author uses these to argue that Muhammad is the foretold prophet.

Key Terms
التوراة
السفر الخامس
طور سيناء
سيعير
فاران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Paran refers to a location in the Sinai Peninsula or elsewhere unrelated to Mecca.
Correction: The author explicitly identifies Paran as a specific place on the road to Mecca, associated with Ishmael and the Arabian desert.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which location in the verse corresponds to the revelation of the Quran?
A) Sinai
B) Seir
C) Paran
D) Mecca

Key Takeaway
The verse from Deuteronomy is interpreted to show that God's revelations came to Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad at Sinai, Seir, and Paran respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This prophecy in the Torah points directly to Muhammad. How does that truth challenge any indifference you might feel toward studying the previous scriptures? What will you do this week to learn more about how the Bible testifies to Islam?
?
The verse connects the three revelations as parts of a single divine plan. Are you living as if you believe this message is the final one, or do you act as if it is just one option among many? What concrete change can you make today to honor the finality of this revelation?
?
Paran is on the road to Mecca—a literal stopping place. Where are you stopping on your spiritual journey? Is there a point in your faith where you have become comfortable and need to move forward? What step will you take this week to proceed toward deeper submission?

A Prophet Like Moses from Among the Brethren
Second prophecy from Deuteronomy about a prophet like Moses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Torah prophecy about a prophet like Moses from 'their brethren' and why it points to Muhammad.

Core Idea
The 'brethren of Israel' are the Ishmaelites, not later Israelite prophets.

The author cites a second prophecy from Deuteronomy where God promises to raise up a prophet like Moses from among the brethren of Israel. This lesson explores the reasoning that identifies this prophet as Muhammad by excluding other candidates.

Guided Path
1
Read the second prophecy
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the promise of a prophet like Moses from 'their brethren'.
This is Deuteronomy 18:18.
2
Analyze the exclusion of other prophets
The author argues that 'brethren' means Ishmaelites, not later Israelite prophets or Jesus. Understand why each alternative is dismissed.
Jesus is not from the brethren because he is from Israel itself, not from the Ishmaelites.
3
Conclude the identification with Muhammad
After excluding others, only Muhammad fits the description as a prophet from the Ishmaelite line, bringing a new law.

The Prophecy of Ishmael's Dominant Descendant
Third prophecy from Genesis about Ishmael's dominant descendant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Torah prophecy about Ishmael's descendant having a dominant hand, and how it points to Muhammad.

Core Idea
The prophecy that Ishmael's hand will be above all is fulfilled in Muhammad's dominance.

The author presents a third Torah prophecy from Genesis, where God tells Abraham that Hagar will bear a son (Ishmael) whose descendants will be dominant. This lesson examines how this is understood as a reference to Muhammad's prophethood and leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the third prophecy
Read the Arabic and translation of the verse from the first book (Genesis). Notice the promise about Ishmael's offspring.
This is Genesis 16:11-12.
2
Interpret the meaning of dominance
The phrase 'his hand above all' and 'all hands stretched out to him in humility' is interpreted as a sign of leadership and universal respect.
3
Connect to Muhammad
The author implies that this prophecy applies to Muhammad, as the most prominent descendant of Ishmael who achieved such status.

Third, Genesis 16:11-12 mentions Ishmael's descendants having the upper hand, which is fulfilled in Muhammad. The author uses these to argue that Muhammad is the foretold prophet.

إبراهيم
هاجر
إسماعيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this prophecy refers to Ishmael himself or to the Arab people in general, not a specific prophet.
Correction: The author specifies that it refers to a particular descendant ('from her offspring will be one') and that this is fulfilled in Muhammad.

Checkpoint

C) Moses

Key Takeaway
The prophecy of a dominant descendant of Ishmael points to Muhammad as the one whose hand is above all.

?
If this prophecy is true, then the message of Islam is confirmed. What are you holding back from sharing this truth with someone who has not heard it? How will you change that this week?

Evidence from the Gospel: The Paraclete as Muhammad
The author cites three Gospel verses about the Paraclete and interprets them as referring to Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Muslim scholars interpreted Gospel references to the Paraclete as prophecies of Muhammad's coming.

Core Idea
The Paraclete in the Gospel is interpreted by Muslim scholars as Muhammad, the revealer of hidden truths.

In this lesson, we examine a key argument from Islamic theological tradition: the claim that the Gospel of John contains prophecies about Muhammad. The author presents three passages from John 14-16 that mention the Paraclete (Fāraqliṭ), which he interprets as meaning 'the revealer of hidden things' and as referring to Muhammad. This forms part of the broader Islamic apologetic for Muhammad's prophethood using earlier scriptures.

Guided Path
1
Read the Gospel quotes
First, read the three quotations from John 14, 15, and 16 as presented in the Arabic text. Notice how each quote describes the Paraclete's role.
The Arabic terms used are important.
2
Understand the author's interpretation
Focus on how the author explains the meaning of 'Fāraqliṭ' and the phrase 'in my name' as referring to prophethood.
The author explicitly states that Fāraqliṭ means 'kāshif al-khafiyyāt' (revealer of hidden things).
3
Reflect on the apologetic significance
Consider why Muslim scholars would use these Gospel passages as evidence for Muhammad's prophethood. How does this argument fit into the broader Muslim-Christian debate?
This is a classic example of interfaith polemics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الإنجيل، فمنها ما ورد في الصحاح الرابع عشر: أنا أطلب لكم إلى أبي حتى يمنحكم ويعطيكم فارقليطا ليكون معكم إلى الأبد. والفارقليط روح الحق واليقين. وفي الخامس عشر: وأما فارقليط روح القدس الذي يرسله أبي باسمي، وهو يعلمكم ويمنحكم جميع الاشياء، وهو يذكركم ما قلته لكم، ثم قال: وإني قد أخبرتكم بهذا قبل أن يكون حتى إذا كان ذلك تؤمنوا به. وقوله: «باسمي» يعني بالنبوة، ومعنى الفارقليط كاشف الخفيات. وفي السادس عشر: أقول لكم الآن حقاً يقيناً أن انطلاقي عنكم خير لكم، فإن لم أنطلق عنكم إلى أبي، لم يأتكم الفارقليط، وإن انطلقت أرسلت به إليكم، فإذا جاء هو يفيد أهل العالم، ويدينهم ويوبخهم، ويوقفهم على الخطيئة والبر. ثم قال: إذا جاء روح الحق واليقين يرشدكم ويعلمكم، ويدبركم جميع الحق، لأنه ليس يتكلم بدعة من تلقاء نفسه.
As for from the Gospel, among them is what came in the fourteenth chapter: 'I ask for you from my Father until He grants you and gives you a Paraclete to be with you forever.' And the Paraclete is the spirit of truth and certainty. And in the fifteenth: 'But the Paraclete, the Holy Spirit, whom my Father will send in my name, He will teach you and grant you all things, and He will remind you of what I said to you.' Then he said: 'And I have told you this before it happens, so that when it happens you may believe in Him.' And His saying 'in my name' means prophethood, and the meaning of al-Fāraqliṭ is the revealer of hidden things. And in the sixteenth: 'I tell you now truly and certainly that my departure from you is better for you; for if I do not depart to my Father, the Paraclete will not come to you. But if I depart, I will send Him to you. And when He comes, He will benefit the people of the world, and judge them, and rebuke them, and make them stop at sin and righteousness.' Then he said: 'When the spirit of truth and certainty comes, He will guide you and teach you, and arrange for you all truth, because He does not speak innovation from himself.'
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents three Gospel quotations about the Paraclete (Fāraqliṭ). The author interprets 'Paraclete' as meaning 'revealer of hidden things' (kāshif al-khafiyyāt). The first quote from John 14 says the Paraclete will be with them forever. The second from John 15 says the Paraclete is the Spirit of Truth sent in the name of Jesus, which the author explains means with prophethood. The third from John 16 says the Paraclete will convict the world of sin and righteousness and guide into all truth, speaking not on his own authority. The author asserts that this refers to Muhammad.

Key Terms
الإنجيل
فارقليط
روح الحق
باسمي
كاشف الخفيات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many Christians believe the Paraclete is the Holy Spirit, a divine person distinct from Jesus.
Correction: The author argues that the Paraclete is a human prophet, Muhammad, who teaches all truth and reveals hidden things.

Checkpoint

B) The revealer of hidden things

D) The lawgiver

Key Takeaway
The author uses Gospel passages about the Paraclete to argue that Jesus foretold the coming of Muhammad as a prophet who reveals hidden truths and guides into all truth.

Prophecies from the Psalms and Scholarly Evidence
The author cites Psalm prophecies and a scholar's collection of scriptural proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two specific prophecies from the Psalms and the scholarly collection of scriptural proofs confirm the prophethood of Muhammad.

Core Idea
Even the Psalms foretold the coming of the maker of the Sunna.

This passage presents two striking prophecies from the Psalms that Muslims interpret as references to Prophet Muhammad. It then strengthens this by citing scholarly works that have collected such scriptural proofs, showing that this evidence is abundant and irrefutable. Let's examine these texts and the scholarly confirmation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Psalm prophecies
Read the two quotes from the Psalms carefully. Notice the imagery of sword and authority in the first, and the explicit prayer for the 'maker of the Sunna' in the second.
The second prophecy explicitly mentions Muhammad by description.
2
Understand the interpretation
Consider how these prophecies are understood to refer to Muhammad. The 'mighty one' and the one who brings a new law (Sunna) are seen as prophetic traits.
3
Note the scholarly confirmation
The author quotes from Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal that such examples are many and cannot be refuted or concealed. Then he mentions Abu al-Husayn al-Basrī's collection of Torah texts.
This shows that the evidence is not limited to the Psalms but spans other scriptures.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Appreciate the claim that opponents cannot refute these texts. Consider how internal consistency across scriptures supports the truth of Muhammad's prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الزبور فقوله تقلد أيها الجبار السيف فإن ناموسك وشرائعك مقرونة بهيئة يمينك، وسهامك مسنونة، والأمم يخرون تحتك. وقوله: قال داود: اللهم ابعث جاعل السنة حتى يعلم الناس أنه بشريعتي أبعث محمداً حتى يعلم الناس أن عيسى بشر. قال في تلخيص المحصل وأمثال هذا كثير في كتب الأنبياء المتقدمين يذكرها المصنفون الواقفون على كتبهم، ولا يقدر المخالف على دفعها أو صرفها إلى ملك أو نبي آخر، ولا على أن يكتمها، ولقد جمع أبو الحسين البصري في كتاب غرر الأدلة ما يوقف من نصوص التوراة على صحة نبوة محمد (عليه السلام).
As for from the Psalms, His saying: 'Gird your sword, O mighty one, for your law and statutes are girded with the glory of your right hand, and your arrows are sharpened, and the nations fall under you.' And His saying: 'David said: O God, send the maker of the Sunna (jāʿil al-sunna) so that people may know that by my law I send Muhammad, so that people may know that Jesus is human.' He said in Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal: 'And examples like this are many in the books of the earlier prophets, mentioned by the authors who are familiar with their books. The opponent cannot refute them or turn them towards another king or prophet, nor can he conceal them. And Abu al-Husayn al-Basrī compiled in his book Ghurar al-Adilla what informs from the texts of the Torah about the truth of the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him).'
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents two prophecies from the Psalms. The first calls upon the mighty one (Muhammad) to gird his sword, alluding to his authority. The second explicitly prays for the 'maker of the Sunna' (jāʿil al-sunna) to be sent, i.e., Muhammad, so that people know that Jesus is human. Then the author quotes from Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal that such examples are abundant in previous scriptures and are irrefutable, and that Abu al-Husayn al-Basrī collected specific Torah texts confirming Muhammad's prophethood in his book Ghurar al-Adilla.

Key Terms
الزبور
جاعل السنة
تلخيص المحصل
أبو الحسين البصري
غرر الأدلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that the phrase 'gird your sword' is a literal command to a warrior rather than a metaphor for prophetic authority.
Correction: In this context, the sword symbolizes divine authority and the prophetic mission, not literal violence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Psalms and earlier scriptures contain explicit references to Muhammad's prophethood, and scholars have collected these to refute opponents.
